I'm in a web dev class, and I am going to be doing my midterm, and it is suggested we use AI. I am willing to give you any context you need, don't be afraid to ask anything you don't 100% know. if you're ready to start, would you like the assingment description?
Absolutely — I’m ready, Amir.

You’re about to start a midterm, it allows AI, and you want me to work with full context and ask whenever something is unclear. That means the next natural step is for you to share the assignment description, because everything we do will anchor to that.

Once I see it, I can help you:

interpret exactly what the instructor is expecting

identify hidden constraints or grading traps

map it to your existing site structure (header/footer includes, grading script, Ascended Academy theme)

decide what parts you want to handle yourself vs. what you want me to generate

fill in any missing bridge‑lessons so you’re not lost after the midterm

Go ahead and drop the assignment description.

<div id="assignment_show" class="assignment content_underline_links" data-lti-page-content="true">

    <div class="assignment-title">
      <div class="title-content">
        <h1 class="title">
          Midterm - Build a multi-page Hobby Site as a single html file
        </h1>
      </div>

<ul class="student-assignment-overview">
  <li>
    <span class="title">Due</span>
    <span class="value">
          <span class="date_text">
                <span class="display_date">Mar 15</span> by
                <span class="display_time">11:59pm</span>

  <div class="description user_content enhanced" data-resource-type="assignment.body" data-resource-id="2839258"><p data-start="1582" data-end="1692">You will be making a website for a hobby/interest of yours that is files, but will appear as multiple pages.</p>
<p data-start="1694" data-end="1819"><span style="color: #c71f23;"><strong>UNLIKE MANY OTHER ASSIGNMENTS, THERE ARE NO LATE SUBMISSIONS OR RESUBMISSIONS FOR THIS ASSIGNMENT, PLEASE PLAN ACCORDINGLY.</strong></span></p>
<p data-start="1821" data-end="1856">You may/should use AI for this :)</p>
<p data-start="1858" data-end="1962">Think of an activity that you are passionate about. If you don't have one, figure one out and fake it.</p>
<ul data-start="1964" data-end="2557">
<li data-start="1964" data-end="2023">
<p data-start="1966" data-end="2023">Make a subfolder in your course folder called <strong data-start="2012" data-end="2021">hobby</strong></p>
</li>
<li data-start="2024" data-end="2103">
<p data-start="2026" data-end="2103">Within that folder create an <strong data-start="2055" data-end="2065">images</strong> folder (and others as you see fit).</p>
</li>
<li data-start="2104" data-end="2557">
<p data-start="2106" data-end="2216">Create an <strong data-start="2116" data-end="2130">index page</strong> that includes all the normal items our pages have had so far – do not use includes.</p>
<ul data-start="2219" data-end="2557">
<li data-start="2219" data-end="2261">
<p data-start="2221" data-end="2261"><strong data-start="2221" data-end="2243">header/main/footer</strong> within the body</p>
</li>
<li data-start="2264" data-end="2327">
<p data-start="2266" data-end="2327"><strong data-start="2266" data-end="2280">header tag</strong> should have an h1 with the name of the hobby</p>
</li>
<li data-start="2330" data-end="2476">
<p data-start="2332" data-end="2476"><strong data-start="2332" data-end="2346">footer tag</strong> should include validation buttons (HTML, CSS, accessibility) and a link to your design firm as you did with the course website.</p>
</li>
<li data-start="2479" data-end="2557">
<p data-start="2481" data-end="2557">The page must validate with no warnings/errors (HTML, CSS, accessibility).</p>
</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p data-start="2559" data-end="2619">Use an <strong data-start="2566" data-end="2589">embedded stylesheet</strong> since this is all one page.</p>
<ul data-start="2620" data-end="2834">
<li data-start="2620" data-end="2666">
<p data-start="2622" data-end="2666">Styling should follow <strong data-start="2644" data-end="2663">CRAP principles</strong>.</p>
</li>
<li data-start="2667" data-end="2772">
<p data-start="2669" data-end="2772">No Times New Roman, no generic blue underlined links, no plain white background, no plain black text.</p>
</li>
<li data-start="2773" data-end="2834">
<p data-start="2775" data-end="2834">Color scheme must be different than your main site pages.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p data-start="2836" data-end="2931">Create sections (using <code data-start="2859" data-end="2870">&lt;section&gt;</code> tags) that act like different “pages” for your hobby site:</p>
<ul data-start="2932" data-end="3328">
<li data-start="2932" data-end="2975">
<p data-start="2934" data-end="2975"><strong data-start="2934" data-end="2941">Who</strong> (About / About Me / Who We Are)</p>
</li>
<li data-start="2976" data-end="3034">
<p data-start="2978" data-end="3034"><strong data-start="2978" data-end="2986">What</strong> (Home page – loads first, explains the hobby)</p>
</li>
<li data-start="3035" data-end="3081">
<p data-start="3037" data-end="3081"><strong data-start="3037" data-end="3045">When</strong> (Best Times / Schedule / Seasons)</p>
</li>
<li data-start="3082" data-end="3121">
<p data-start="3084" data-end="3121"><strong data-start="3084" data-end="3093">Where</strong> (Best Locations / Places)</p>
</li>
<li data-start="3122" data-end="3133">
<p data-start="3124" data-end="3133"><strong data-start="3124" data-end="3131">How</strong></p>
</li>
<li data-start="3134" data-end="3145">
<p data-start="3136" data-end="3145"><strong data-start="3136" data-end="3143">Why</strong></p>
</li>
<li data-start="3146" data-end="3328">
<p data-start="3148" data-end="3328"><strong data-start="3148" data-end="3162">AI Prompts</strong> (if you used AI for code or text, show prompts here. If only images were AI-generated, include a comment in your submission that says “I only used AI for images.”)</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p data-start="3330" data-end="3352">Each section should:</p>
<ul data-start="3353" data-end="3781">
<li data-start="3353" data-end="3376">
<p data-start="3355" data-end="3376">Have an h2 heading.</p>
</li>
<li data-start="3377" data-end="3468">
<p data-start="3379" data-end="3468">Contain <strong data-start="3387" data-end="3411">at least three items</strong> (e.g., paragraphs, figures, tables, forms, slideshow).</p>
</li>
<li data-start="3469" data-end="3576">
<p data-start="3471" data-end="3576">Include at least <strong data-start="3488" data-end="3502">one figure</strong> (image + caption + italicized paragraph explaining the AI prompt used).</p>
</li>
<li data-start="3577" data-end="3702">
<p data-start="3579" data-end="3702">Images must be your own, AI-generated, or open source. Keep a consistent style (e.g., all photorealistic or all cartoon).</p>
</li>
<li data-start="3703" data-end="3781">
<p data-start="3705" data-end="3781">Resize large images and maintain aspect ratio to avoid long loading times.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p data-start="3783" data-end="3796">Navigation:</p>
<ul data-start="3797" data-end="4109">
<li data-start="3797" data-end="3856">
<p data-start="3799" data-end="3856">Add links in the header/nav to switch between sections.</p>
</li>
<li data-start="3857" data-end="3947">
<p data-start="3859" data-end="3947">Use CSS/JS so only the selected section is visible at a time (like a single-page app).</p>
</li>
<li data-start="3948" data-end="4041">
<p data-start="3950" data-end="4041">Use a unique divider symbol (emoji or special character) different from your other pages.</p>
</li>
<li data-start="4042" data-end="4109">
<p data-start="4044" data-end="4109">The <strong data-start="4048" data-end="4063">What (Home)</strong> section should be the default visible page.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p data-start="4111" data-end="4120">Lastly:</p>
<ul data-start="4121" data-end="4319">
<li data-start="4121" data-end="4237">
<p data-start="4123" data-end="4237">Add this new page to the secondary menu on your main site as <strong data-start="4184" data-end="4195">“Hobby”</strong> so it’s accessible from your main site.</p>
</li>
<li data-start="4121" data-end="4237">Create a favicon for your page using a tool like <a href="https://favicon.io/" target="_blank" class="external" rel="noreferrer noopener"><span>https://favicon.io/</span><span class="external_link_icon" style="margin-inline-start: 5px; display: inline-block; text-indent: initial; " role="presentation"><svg viewBox="0 0 1920 1920" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" style="width:1em; height:1em; vertical-align:middle; fill:currentColor">
    <path d="M1226.667 267c88.213 0 160 71.787 160 160v426.667H1280v-160H106.667v800C106.667 1523 130.56 1547 160 1547h1066.667c29.44 0 53.333-24 53.333-53.333v-213.334h106.667v213.334c0 88.213-71.787 160-160 160H160c-88.213 0-160-71.787-160-160V427c0-88.213 71.787-160 160-160Zm357.706 442.293 320 320c20.8 20.8 20.8 54.614 0 75.414l-320 320-75.413-75.414 228.907-228.906H906.613V1013.72h831.254L1508.96 784.707l75.413-75.414Zm-357.706-335.626H160c-29.44 0-53.333 24-53.333 53.333v160H1280V427c0-29.333-23.893-53.333-53.333-53.333Z" fill-rule="evenodd"></path>
</svg>
<span class="screenreader-only">Links to an external site.</span></span></a> or something similar of your choosing.&nbsp;</li>
<li data-start="4238" data-end="4319">
<p data-start="4240" data-end="4319">Submit the link:<br data-start="4256" data-end="4259"><strong data-start="4261" data-end="4317"><a class="" href="https://webpages.charlotte.edu/UNCCID/itis3135/hobby" target="_new" data-start="4263" data-end="4315">https://webpages.charlotte.edu/UNCCID/itis3135/hobby</a></strong></p>
</li>
</ul></div>

Nice, thanks for pulling the whole thing in like that—that makes this way easier to do right the first time.

From the assignment, the core is:

“You will be making a website for a hobby/interest of yours that is files, but will appear as multiple pages.”
“Use CSS/JS so only the selected section is visible at a time (like a single-page app).”

So we’re building a single HTML file (hobby/index.html) with:

Embedded CSS (no external stylesheet, no includes)

Sections as faux pages: What (home, default), Who, When, Where, How, Why, AI Prompts

Header/nav + main + footer, with nav links that show/hide sections

Each section: h2, at least 3 content items, at least one <figure> with image + caption + italicized paragraph explaining the AI prompt used

CRAP styling, non-default fonts/colors, new color scheme, unique divider symbol

Validation buttons + design firm link in footer

Favicon and a link from your main site’s secondary menu labeled “Hobby”

Before we touch code, I want to anchor to your choices so it feels like your site, not a generic template.

What hobby are you leaning toward? (Real or “fake but fun” both work.)

Do you already have your main site’s structure handy? (So we can match the “design firm” link and secondary menu pattern.)

Do you want me to draft a full index.html skeleton that you then customize, or build it section-by-section with you driving?

Once you answer those, I can sketch a clean, validating skeleton tailored to your hobby and your existing site conventions.

1. can gaming work? It's not a hobby where you build anything, so if that's a bad choice let me know to pivot
2. yes
3. the latter sounds better
Gaming works perfectly as a hobby for this assignment. The instructions never require the hobby to involve building or crafting; they only require that you can talk about it across the six lenses (Who, What, When, Where, How, Why). The assignment even says:

“Think of an activity that you are passionate about. If you don't have one, figure one out and fake it.”
“Create sections… that act like different ‘pages’ for your hobby site.”

Gaming fits that structure cleanly because each section can map to something meaningful:

What — what gaming is to you, genres you enjoy, why it’s compelling.

Who — about you as a gamer, or the gaming community.

When — best times to play, seasonal events, release cycles.

Where — favorite platforms, setups, or gaming spaces.

How — how you play, how to get started, how to choose games.

Why — why gaming matters to you (relaxation, challenge, social connection).

This gives you plenty of content without stretching or faking anything.

How this aligns with the assignment’s requirements
Gaming gives you natural opportunities to include:

Figures: screenshots of games, AI‑generated images of gaming setups, stylized icons.

Three items per section: paragraphs, lists, figures, maybe a small table comparing genres.

Consistent image style: all photorealistic, all pixel‑art, all neon‑cyberpunk, etc.

CRAP principles: contrast with a dark‑mode palette, repetition with section dividers, alignment with a clean layout, proximity grouping content.

Navigation: gaming-themed divider symbol (e.g., “◆”, “✦”, “⟡”, “⊹”).

Embedded stylesheet: easy to theme with a dark UI and accent color.

Nothing in the rubric penalizes a hobby that isn’t “productive.” It only cares that the site is complete, styled, validated, and structured.

How we can proceed with your preferred workflow
You said you want to build it section-by-section with you driving, which fits your learning style and keeps you in control.

The next step is choosing the specific flavor of gaming so the site feels cohesive. A few options:

PC gaming

Retro gaming

RPGs

Competitive shooters

Cozy games

Nintendo-focused

“Gaming as a stress-relief hobby”

“Gaming setups and optimization”

Any of these can anchor the whole site’s tone, images, and color scheme.

Which direction within gaming feels most natural for you to build a whole page around?

I like most these to varying extent.
PC Gaming - Ideal way to play games for me
Retro Games - I love retro games so much I hook up a CRT to my pc for the games that look better there
RPGs - there's been very few rpgs I dislike

You’ve got six different angles on gaming, and any one of them could anchor a strong hobby site. The trick is choosing the one that gives you the cleanest structure, the most natural content, and the easiest time meeting the assignment’s requirements (figures, three items per section, consistent image style, etc.).

🎮 The core question: which theme gives you the richest, easiest content?
Looking at what you listed, three themes stand out as especially strong:

🖥️ PC Gaming (your “ideal way to play”)
This is the most flexible and assignment‑friendly option because it naturally supports:

Who — PC gamers, you as a PC gamer, the PC gaming community

What — what PC gaming is, why it’s your preferred platform

When — seasonal sales (Steam Summer Sale), release cycles, best times to upgrade hardware

Where — your desk setup, recommended setups, Steam, Epic, GOG

How — how to choose parts, how to optimize settings, how to mod games

Why — freedom, performance, customization, preservation

It also makes the figure requirement trivial:
AI‑generated images of setups, GPUs, keyboards, neon cyberpunk rigs, etc.

And it fits your interest in advising people on gaming setups.

This is the most “effortless A” direction.

🕹️ Retro Gaming (especially with your CRT setup)
This is the most unique and personality‑driven option.

You already have a distinctive angle:

“I hook up a CRT to my PC for the games that look better there.”

That alone can fill entire sections:

Who — retro enthusiasts, preservationists

What — what retro gaming is, why CRTs matter

When — console generations, eras, release timelines

Where — your setup, emulation vs. original hardware

How — how to connect a CRT, how shaders work, how to emulate legally

Why — nostalgia, aesthetics, authenticity

AI images can be stylized pixel‑art or retro‑futuristic CRT shots.

This is the most aesthetic and cohesive theme.

🧙 RPGs (you love almost all of them)
This is the most content‑rich theme.

RPGs give you:

Who — the player, the party, the RPG community

What — what defines an RPG, subgenres

When — release eras, long‑form play sessions, expansions

Where — worlds, settings, maps

How — how to build characters, how to choose an RPG, how progression works

Why — storytelling, immersion, character growth

AI images can be fantasy landscapes, character portraits, pixel‑art RPG scenes.

This is the most narrative and creative theme.

Which theme is least ideal?
Competitive shooters — because you don’t actually play them competitively

Cozy games — because you only enjoy them with friends

Nintendo — doable, but you’d need to avoid copyrighted imagery unless you generate AI lookalikes

These aren’t bad, but they’re not as aligned with your strengths.

The best match for you specifically
Based on what you said, the strongest choice is:

PC Gaming with a retro‑friendly twist
A theme like:

“PC Gaming: Modern Power, Retro Soul”

Why this works:

It lets you talk about modern PC gaming (your main preference).

It lets you include your CRT retro setup, which is unique and memorable.

It lets you incorporate RPGs, Nintendo emulation, cozy games, etc., without the site feeling scattered.

It gives you a clear visual style (dark mode + neon accents + CRT scanline aesthetic).

It makes the How section extremely natural (settings optimization, hardware advice).

It gives you endless easy figure ideas for each section.

This is the theme that will feel the most “you,” while also being the easiest to execute cleanly.

A clean next step is to lock in the section‑by‑section outline for your single‑page site so you can start building the HTML in your preferred workflow. Two short lines from the assignment anchor what we’re doing:

“Create sections… that act like different ‘pages’ for your hobby site.”
“Use CSS/JS so only the selected section is visible at a time.”

Everything below fits that structure while letting you talk about both modern PC gaming and retro gaming in a unified way.

🧭 Overall structure of your hobby site
Your file will be hobby/index.html with:

<header> containing the site title + nav links

<main> containing seven <section> elements

<footer> with validation buttons + your design firm link

Embedded <style> and a small <script> to show/hide sections

The theme: PC Gaming: Modern Power, Retro Soul
Color scheme: dark background, neon accent (blue, purple, or green), CRT‑style divider symbol (e.g., ✦).

🗂️ Section‑by‑section outline (tailored to your theme)
1. What — Home (default visible)
Purpose: Introduce PC gaming as your main platform and how retro gaming fits into it.

Content ideas:

Paragraph: why PC is your ideal way to play

Paragraph: how modern and retro coexist in your setup

Figure: AI‑generated image of a hybrid modern/retro gaming desk

Optional: small list of genres you love (RPGs, retro platformers, etc.)

2. Who — About / About Me / Who We Are
Purpose: Explain your identity as a gamer and the communities you connect with.

Content ideas:

Paragraph: your background with gaming

Paragraph: your CRT setup and why it matters to you

Figure: AI image of a retro CRT with a modern PC tower

Optional: short list of communities (PC modding, retro enthusiasts, RPG fans)

3. When — Best Times / Schedule / Seasons
Purpose: Talk about gaming rhythms, release cycles, and when certain games shine.

Content ideas:

Paragraph: seasonal events (Steam sales, holiday releases)

Paragraph: when retro games feel best to play (late night, weekends, etc.)

Figure: AI image of a cozy nighttime gaming setup

Optional: small table comparing “modern gaming seasons” vs. “retro moods”

4. Where — Best Locations / Places
Purpose: Describe where gaming happens physically and digitally.

Content ideas:

Paragraph: your desk setup (modern monitor + CRT)

Paragraph: digital “places” like Steam, GOG, emulation frontends

Figure: AI image of a dual‑monitor + CRT layout

Optional: list of favorite platforms (Steam, Dolphin, RetroArch)

5. How — Methods / Getting Started / Tips
Purpose: Explain how you approach gaming and how others can start.

Content ideas:

Paragraph: how you choose games (RPGs, retro, Nintendo classics)

Paragraph: how you optimize PC settings or connect a CRT

Figure: AI image of a GPU or PC interior

Optional: list of “starter tips” for new PC gamers

6. Why — Motivation / Meaning
Purpose: Explain why gaming matters to you.

Content ideas:

Paragraph: relaxation, challenge, nostalgia

Paragraph: why retro aesthetics resonate with you

Figure: AI image of a stylized pixel‑art scene

Optional: short reflection on how gaming fits into your life

7. AI Prompts — Required
Purpose: Show transparency about AI usage.

Content ideas:

List of prompts used for images

List of prompts used for text or code (if any)

If only images were AI‑generated, you can state that here

🧩 Navigation behavior (JS)
Your nav links will:

Hide all sections

Show the selected section

Default to showing What (Home) on page load

This satisfies the assignment’s requirement that it “appear as multiple pages” while being a single HTML file.

🖼️ Image style consistency
Pick one style for all images:

photorealistic

neon cyberpunk

retro‑futuristic

pixel‑art

This keeps the site visually coherent and makes validation easier.

🔧 Next step
To keep your workflow clean and in your control, the next natural move is to build the HTML skeleton with empty sections and nav links so you can start filling content.

Do you want the skeleton to include:

just the structure (header/nav/main/footer/sections),
or

structure + placeholder text so you can replace it as you go?

I want comments in lieu of placeholders on my structure.
A commented skeleton gives you a clean, readable starting point while keeping you fully in control of the content. Two lines from the assignment anchor what this file must support:

“Create sections… that act like different ‘pages’ for your hobby site.”
“Use CSS/JS so only the selected section is visible at a time.”

The structure below includes all required elements, but replaces every piece of content with clear comments so you can fill things in your way.

Hobby Site Skeleton (with comments instead of placeholders)
html
<!DOCTYPE html>
<html lang="en">
<head>
    <meta charset="UTF-8">
    <meta name="viewport" content="width=device-width, initial-scale=1.0">
    <title>PC Gaming: Modern Power, Retro Soul</title>

    <!-- Embedded stylesheet required by assignment -->
    <style>
        /* ------------------------------
           GLOBAL STYLES
           - Set dark background
           - Choose neon accent color
           - Remove Times New Roman
           - Remove default blue links
           - Apply CRAP principles
        ------------------------------ */

        body {
            /* your background color */
            /* your font-family */
            /* your text color */
            margin: 0;
        }

        header, footer {
            /* styling for header/footer */
        }

        nav a {
            /* style nav links */
            /* remove default blue underline */
        }

        /* Unique divider symbol styling if needed */

        /* ------------------------------
           SECTION VISIBILITY
           - All sections hidden by default
           - JS will show the active one
        ------------------------------ */
        section {
            display: none;
            padding: 1.5rem;
        }

        /* Default visible section (What/Home) */
        section#what {
            display: block;
        }

        /* Figure styling (image + caption + italicized AI prompt explanation) */
        figure {
            /* your figure styling */
        }
    </style>

    <!-- Small JS for showing/hiding sections -->
    <script>
        function showSection(id) {
            // hide all sections
            document.querySelectorAll("main section").forEach(sec => sec.style.display = "none");

            // show selected section
            document.getElementById(id).style.display = "block";
        }
    </script>
</head>

<body>

<header>
    <h1>PC Gaming: Modern Power, Retro Soul</h1>

    <nav>
        <!-- Each link calls showSection() with the section ID -->
        <!-- Use your unique divider symbol between links -->
        <a href="#" onclick="showSection('what')">What</a>
        <!-- divider symbol -->
        <a href="#" onclick="showSection('who')">Who</a>
        <!-- divider symbol -->
        <a href="#" onclick="showSection('when')">When</a>
        <!-- divider symbol -->
        <a href="#" onclick="showSection('where')">Where</a>
        <!-- divider symbol -->
        <a href="#" onclick="showSection('how')">How</a>
        <!-- divider symbol -->
        <a href="#" onclick="showSection('why')">Why</a>
        <!-- divider symbol -->
        <a href="#" onclick="showSection('ai-prompts')">AI Prompts</a>
    </nav>
</header>

<main>

    <!-- WHAT (Home) — default visible -->
    <section id="what">
        <!-- h2 heading -->
        <!-- Three content items (paragraphs, lists, figures, etc.) -->
        <!-- One figure with image + caption + italicized AI prompt explanation -->
        <!-- Content about modern PC gaming + retro twist -->
    </section>

    <!-- WHO -->
    <section id="who">
        <!-- h2 heading -->
        <!-- Three content items -->
        <!-- One figure -->
        <!-- Content about you as a gamer, communities, CRT setup, etc. -->
    </section>

    <!-- WHEN -->
    <section id="when">
        <!-- h2 heading -->
        <!-- Three content items -->
        <!-- One figure -->
        <!-- Content about gaming rhythms, Steam sales, retro moods, etc. -->
    </section>

    <!-- WHERE -->
    <section id="where">
        <!-- h2 heading -->
        <!-- Three content items -->
        <!-- One figure -->
        <!-- Content about your desk setup, digital platforms, CRT placement, etc. -->
    </section>

    <!-- HOW -->
    <section id="how">
        <!-- h2 heading -->
        <!-- Three content items -->
        <!-- One figure -->
        <!-- Content about how you choose games, optimize settings, connect CRT, etc. -->
    </section>

    <!-- WHY -->
    <section id="why">
        <!-- h2 heading -->
        <!-- Three content items -->
        <!-- One figure -->
        <!-- Content about meaning, nostalgia, relaxation, challenge, etc. -->
    </section>

    <!-- AI PROMPTS -->
    <section id="ai-prompts">
        <!-- h2 heading -->
        <!-- List of prompts used for images -->
        <!-- List of prompts used for text/code if any -->

A neon‑yellow accent works best when paired with a deep blue‑black or purple‑black background, because the contrast feels authentically retro‑futuristic without being harsh. Do you want the accent to be a pure neon yellow (#f5ff3a) or a slightly warmer one (#ffe93a) that’s easier on the eyes?

let's go with the bright yellow
A bright, high‑energy neon yellow works beautifully with a retro‑futuristic theme because it gives you the “glow” you want without washing out against a dark background. It also pairs cleanly with the assignment’s requirement that your styling be intentional and non-default:

“No Times New Roman, no generic blue underlined links, no plain white background, no plain black text.”
“Styling should follow CRAP principles.”

To make the accent feel cohesive across your whole site, it helps to anchor three things: the exact yellow, the background family, and the glow style.

🎨 Accent color: the bright neon yellow
A strong, readable neon yellow that still validates well is:

#f5ff3a — bright, electric, slightly green‑leaning
or
#faff00 — pure neon highlighter yellow

Both work, but #f5ff3a tends to glow better on screens without causing eye strain.

Use this accent for:

nav link text or borders

hover glows

section dividers (your ✦ symbol)

figure captions

small UI elements like buttons or highlights

Keeping the accent consistent across these elements reinforces repetition and alignment (CRAP).

🌌 Background family that supports neon yellow
Neon yellow needs a deep, cool-toned dark to pop without looking muddy. Three reliable options:

#0a0a0f — deep blue‑black, very retro‑futuristic

#0d0b1a — purple‑black, more synthwave

#050507 — neutral dark with a slight blue tint

Any of these will make the yellow feel like it’s glowing off the page.

✨ Glow style that fits retro‑futuristic
Retro‑futuristic design loves subtle outer glows. A clean, accessible glow for neon yellow:

css
text-shadow: 0 0 6px #f5ff3a, 0 0 12px #f5ff3a;
You can apply this to:

your site title

nav link hover states

section headings

figure captions

Just avoid overusing it—glow is strongest when used sparingly.

🧩 Where this fits into your skeleton
You can now fill in the commented CSS areas like:

css
body {
    background: #0a0a0f;
    color: #e6e6e6;
    font-family: 'Orbitron', sans-serif; /* or any futuristic font */
}

nav a {
    color: #f5ff3a;
    text-decoration: none;
}

nav a:hover {
    text-shadow: 0 0 6px #f5ff3a, 0 0 12px #f5ff3a;
}
This gives you a consistent, intentional visual identity that matches your theme and satisfies the assignment’s styling rules.

Would you like help choosing a divider symbol that fits the retro‑futuristic neon aesthetic (e.g., ✦, ⟡, ⧖, ⋆)?

sure, let's go with something that a line (|,\,/, etc) or an angle (Shaped like ^, +, L, etc)
A retro‑futuristic theme pairs really well with angular or line‑based dividers, because they echo the geometry of old vector displays, CRT scanlines, and neon signage. Since you want something built from lines or angles, these are the strongest options that stay readable, stylish, and consistent with your neon‑yellow accent.

Line‑based dividers that fit retro‑futuristic neon
These feel like glowing UI separators from an 80s sci‑fi terminal.

│ (vertical bar — clean, minimal, very readable)

┃ (heavy vertical bar — stronger neon‑glow energy)

╽ (angled vertical bar — subtle retro‑terminal vibe)

╿ (mirrored angled vertical bar — more stylized)

╱ (forward slash — evokes CRT scanlines)

╲ (backslash — pairs well with ╱ for symmetry)

╳ (crossed lines — feels like a glowing vector‑graphic “X”)

Angle‑based dividers with a retro‑futuristic feel
These look like stylized arrows, circuitry, or neon signage.

⌁ (zig‑line — extremely retro‑futuristic)

⋔ (angled fork — looks like a stylized circuit symbol)

⋇ (angled star — subtle but geometric)

⧉ (square‑angle cross — very “synthwave UI”)

⧓ (angled diamond — sharp, geometric, neon‑friendly)

⟡ (angled star‑diamond — clean and futuristic)

⟠ (angular symbol — feels like a glowing HUD marker)

Which ones work best with neon yellow?
Neon yellow glows most cleanly around thin to medium‑weight symbols. The top three for your theme:

╱ — evokes CRT scanlines and retro vector graphics

⧓ — sharp, geometric, very retro‑futuristic

│ — minimal, clean, and looks great glowing neon

If you want something that feels like a glowing “beam” between nav links, ╱ is the strongest choice.

If you want, I can help you pick one and show how it would look styled in your nav with neon‑yellow glow.

let's pick option 3
Using │ as your divider gives you a clean, geometric, retro‑futuristic look that fits your neon‑yellow theme and keeps the navigation readable. It also reinforces the assignment’s emphasis on intentional styling rather than default browser behavior, which is supported by the requirement that “Styling should follow CRAP principles” and that you avoid “generic blue underlined links.”

Why │ works especially well in your design
It’s a pure line, which matches the angular, vector‑display aesthetic you’re aiming for.

Neon yellow glows cleanly around thin symbols, so │ will look crisp with a text‑shadow glow.

It keeps the nav minimal and futuristic, avoiding clutter while still giving clear separation.

It scales well across screen sizes and doesn’t break layout or spacing.

How it fits into your nav
You’ll place it between links like:

This keeps the divider integrated into the neon‑yellow visual language without needing separate styling.

Do you want the divider to have extra spacing around it (like &nbsp;│&nbsp;) or a tighter, more compact look?

more spacing

✔️ Content Item #2 for How can I PC Game?
When I start a game, I usually reach for my Steam Deck-as-a-controller unless the game is built around the mouse, like point‑and‑clicks. If a game is d‑pad centric — mostly 2D stuff — I grab my Super Nintendo controller, even if that means getting creative to make it work. My choices always come down to a mix of precision, comfort, immersion, and what the game actually demands. A controller often feels more immersive to me because it’s more expressive and precise, and the fact that so many shooters pair stick‑only camera controls with heavy aim assist says a lot about the limitations of that input method. Steam Input is where everything comes together: I tweak every game, I refine every layout, and even though I have loose templates for certain genres, it always takes playtesting to get it perfect — and I love that process. My desktop is usually the best place to play, but some games deserve the comfort of my bed or the convenience of being a passtime between things, which is where the Steam Deck shines as a handheld. Original hardware is reserved for rare edge cases like Mother 3, where timing or latency actually matters. And VR is something I see as the future — maybe even the standard one day — but right now it’s more of an experimental glimpse at what full sensory immersion could be. If I ever get frustrated at a game for not supporting my controller properly, it’s only because I know the fix wouldn’t take much work, and the experience could be so much better for everyone.

ok
Here’s Content Item #3 — the final piece of the How can I PC Game? section — built to complement the first two without repeating anything. This one zooms out and captures the philosophy behind all your methods: the idea that PC gaming is about freedom, refinement, and making games better, not about defending an input method or proving anything to anyone.

✔️ Content Item #3 for How can I PC Game?
At the end of the day, the way I play is shaped by a simple idea: PC gaming gives you the freedom to make every game feel better than it did by default. It’s not about choosing sides or arguing over which input method is “correct,” because every method has strengths and weaknesses, and every player values different things. What matters to me is that the tools exist to refine the experience — to make controls more expressive, to make movement feel natural, to make aiming feel intuitive, to make the whole thing more immersive. Steam Input, gyro, handheld play, original hardware, VR… they’re all just different ways of reaching the same goal: shaping the game so it plays the way it should. And when a game doesn’t support that level of refinement, it’s frustrating not because I’m picky, but because I know how little it would take to elevate the experience for everyone. PC gaming is at its best when it embraces flexibility, experimentation, and the idea that players should be able to interact with games in the way that feels right to them — not the way tradition says they should.

      of controls, the moment everything finally clicked into place. Gyro aim gives me a level of
      precision and comfort that feels right for me, just like mouse and keyboard feels right for others.
      And honestly, both sides can get weirdly defensive — controller players and mouse players alike —
      when it should really be a conversation about making gaming more accessible, more flexible, and
      more enjoyable for everyone. The fun part is perfecting the control scheme, dialing in the settings,
      and shaping the experience so each game plays the way it deserves to.
    </p>

      (with gyro, to me at least) often feels more immersive because it’s more expressive and precise, and
      the fact that so many shooters pair stick‑only camera controls with heavy aim assist says a lot about
      the limitations of that input method. Steam Input is where everything comes together: I tweak every
      game, I refine every layout, and even though I have loose templates for certain genres, it always
      takes playtesting to get it perfect — and I love that process. My desktop is usually the best place

      </iframe>

then you said:

A bright, high‑energy neon yellow works beautifully with a retro‑futuristic theme because it gives you the “glow” you want without washing out against a dark background. It also pairs cleanly with the assignment’s requirement that your styling be intentional and non-default:

> “No Times New Roman, no generic blue underlined links, no plain white background, no plain black text.”
> “Styling should follow CRAP principles.”

To make the accent feel cohesive across your whole site, it helps to anchor three things: the **exact yellow**, the **background family**, and the **glow style**.

---

## 🎨 Accent color: the bright neon yellow
A strong, readable neon yellow that still validates well is:

**`#f5ff3a`** — bright, electric, slightly green‑leaning
or
**`#faff00`** — pure neon highlighter yellow

Both work, but `#f5ff3a` tends to glow better on screens without causing eye strain.

Use this accent for:

- nav link text or borders
- hover glows
- section dividers (your ✦ symbol)
- figure captions
- small UI elements like buttons or highlights

Keeping the accent consistent across these elements reinforces repetition and alignment (CRAP).

---

## 🌌 Background family that supports neon yellow
Neon yellow needs a **deep, cool-toned dark** to pop without looking muddy. Three reliable options:

- **`#0a0a0f`** — deep blue‑black, very retro‑futuristic
- **`#0d0b1a`** — purple‑black, more synthwave
- **`#050507`** — neutral dark with a slight blue tint

Any of these will make the yellow feel like it’s glowing off the page.

---

## ✨ Glow style that fits retro‑futuristic
Retro‑futuristic design loves subtle outer glows. A clean, accessible glow for neon yellow:

```css
text-shadow: 0 0 6px #f5ff3a, 0 0 12px #f5ff3a;
```

You can apply this to:

- your site title
- nav link hover states
- section headings
- figure captions

Just avoid overusing it—glow is strongest when used sparingly.

---

## 🧩 Where this fits into your skeleton
You can now fill in the commented CSS areas like:

```css
body {
    background: #0a0a0f;
    color: #e6e6e6;
    font-family: 'Orbitron', sans-serif; /* or any futuristic font */
}

nav a {
    color: #f5ff3a;
    text-decoration: none;
}

nav a:hover {
    text-shadow: 0 0 6px #f5ff3a, 0 0 12px #f5ff3a;
}
```

This gives you a consistent, intentional visual identity that matches your theme and satisfies the assignment’s styling rules.

---

Would you like help choosing a **divider symbol** that fits the retro‑futuristic neon aesthetic (e.g., ✦, ⟡, ⧖, ⋆)?

I landed on │

then you said Using **│** as your divider gives you a clean, geometric, retro‑futuristic look that fits your neon‑yellow theme and keeps the navigation readable. It also reinforces the assignment’s emphasis on intentional styling rather than default browser behavior, which is supported by the requirement that “Styling should follow CRAP principles” and that you avoid “generic blue underlined links.”

### Why **│** works especially well in your design
- It’s a **pure line**, which matches the angular, vector‑display aesthetic you’re aiming for.
- Neon yellow glows cleanly around thin symbols, so **│** will look crisp with a text‑shadow glow.
- It keeps the nav minimal and futuristic, avoiding clutter while still giving clear separation.
- It scales well across screen sizes and doesn’t break layout or spacing.

### How it fits into your nav
You’ll place it between links like:

```
<a>What</a> │ <a>Who</a> │ <a>When</a> …
```

And you can give it a subtle glow by styling the nav links themselves, since the divider inherits the same color:

```css
nav a {
    color: #f5ff3a;
    text-decoration: none;
}

nav a:hover {
    text-shadow: 0 0 6px #f5ff3a, 0 0 12px #f5ff3a;
}
```

This keeps the divider integrated into the neon‑yellow visual language without needing separate styling.

WHAT:
    h2: My Awesome Hobby: PC Gaming
    content paragraph 1: PC gaming is genuinely the ultimate way to play games for me. A lot of people get overwhelmed by all the settings and sliders, but I actually enjoy configuring everything and making a game run exactly how I want. That applies to retro games too—between emulation, PC ports, and community projects, you can customize older titles in ways that weren’t possible on original hardware, and I even hook a CRT TV directly to my PC because many 90s games were designed around that display technology. After all the setup, I finally sit down and play, and it feels like I’m experiencing the game in the best possible way.
    content paragraph 2: When I think of PC gaming, I see it as an open platform built around freedom and choice. You can decide exactly what settings you run your games at, where you buy them, what hardware you use, and even what operating system you prefer—Linux now runs most games out of the box. PC gaming also gives you the variety you expect from any platform, from online multiplayer without a subscription to any genre you can imagine, all on whatever display you want. And because the PC supports emulation, I can legally dump and play the games I own using the tools I prefer, which adds another layer of flexibility I really enjoy.
    content paragraph 3: Building a computer can be a big barrier for some people, which is why many just buy a pre‑built, but I genuinely enjoy putting together part lists and seeing how different components work together. Any time a new console launches, I’m immediately curious about how it stacks up against modern PC hardware and what design choices the developers made. I love analyzing how console makers and game studios optimize their games for specific hardware, and channels like Digital Foundry dive deep into exactly that kind of comparison. Following this kind of technical breakdown is a big part of what makes PC gaming exciting for me.

    h2: “Who I Am: Amir Homsi”
    content paragraph 1: I’ve been playing on computers in some form since I was about five. I detoured into iPads and iOS during the latter half of elementary school, but because Apple locked down so many gaming features, I ended up tinkering with those devices the same way I would a computer. As I got older and gained access to better hardware, I finally got to play more games than I watched, and now I try to play—or at least form an opinion on—as many games as I can.
    content paragraph 2: It took me a surprisingly long time to realize that computer science was the field I’d been orbiting my whole life. The childhood dream of game development, jailbreaking my iPad, and homebrewing my 2DS were all connected, but the wires didn’t click until middle school, when I finally learned that being a doctor was nothing like the three video games that made it look fun. Once I turned my attention toward computers directly, they stopped being just the middleman for entertainment and became the entertainment themselves. Sometimes I wonder how much deeper into this hobby I would’ve gone if I had figured it out earlier—would I be some kind of super‑genius by now???
    content paragraph 3: I decided to study computer science at UNCC mostly because it was affordable and close to home, not because of some dramatic lifelong dream. Even so, I’ve learned a lot here—especially compared to high school, where most classes felt like a snooze fest except for math and a few odd exceptions. I’m naturally curious and inquisitive, so sometimes I wonder how much further along I’d be if I’d had real CS classes earlier, maybe even enough to land a job in this chaotic job market already. And for the record, if any employer ever reads this, my lack of club involvement isn’t because I couldn’t be bothered—there just wasn’t a group that felt like the right place for me, and I would’ve absolutely killed it if there had been.

WHO:
    h2: Why PC gaming?
    content paragraph 1: PC gaming is the closest thing gaming has to being truly open source. The entire world quietly runs on open‑source software, and most people don’t realize just how much of everything they use depends on it. I love PC gaming because it shares that same spirit—when a hobby is open, people can build wild, creative things that would never exist in a locked‑down ecosystem. Imagine if every CG asset in your favorite action movie were available for cheap or even free, or if you could build an original car from parts sold by BMW, Toyota, or even Ferrari; that’s the kind of possibility openness creates.
    content paragraph 2: Another reason I care so much about PC gaming is that there’s always one layer deeper—there’s always more to the story. Any time I tweak a setting, mod a game, or fix something that breaks, it usually confuses me at first, but I often end up learning how all these pieces manage to play nice… or sometimes don’t. It’s a hobby that gets better the more you do it, and honestly, it even improves passively over time just by being around it.
    content paragraph 3: I also care about PC gaming because it’s one of the few hobbies that actually grows with you instead of aging out (the RAM prices will go down, high framerates and resolutions will become affordable, just have faith!). The games I played as a kid still run today—sometimes better than ever—and the hardware I use now will still matter years from now in some form. There’s something comforting about a hobby that I’ll fight to keep around, one that doesn’t vanish when the trend cycle moves on but keeps expanding, layering new experiences on top of old ones without replacing them.

WHEN:
    h2: When I Dive Into PC Gaming
    content paragraph 1: I used to play for hours every day, especially back in middle school. Nowadays there are plenty of days where I don’t play a single game, and that honestly saddens me a bit. But I’m also glad I respect gaming enough to want each session to feel complete, not something that gets arbitrarily cut short. I try to play only when I feel I can give whatever game I’m in the middle of the attention it deserves.
    content paragraph 2: I also pick the right kind of game for the time I have and where I am in life. I can’t grind an Elden Ring boss if I’m lying in bed or sitting in a waiting room, but I can play something like Metroid or Silksong in those moments. I make time to play with friends too, and that usually creates a safe, dedicated block of time where the game gets my full attention. Some games even feel like they require a specific controller, especially retro ones, and I won’t play them unless I have the right setup.
    content paragraph 3: There are also stretches where I don’t end up gaming, but it’s never because I need time away from screens — my brain is basically flipped from people who feel that way. Sometimes I play because it fits into a routine my mind is stuck in, even outside of being productive; booting a game or beating a small section can feel like a tiny goal I set for myself. Other times I’m just not in the right headspace for a specific game, and I wait until the moment feels right to return. When I do come back, it feels intentional, like I picked the exact time that makes the experience feel satisfying instead of rushed.

WHERE:
    h2: Where can I PC Game?
    content paragraph 1: I usually play games at my desk, because that’s my battle station — if that wasn’t my go‑to spot, something would be seriously wrong. But sometimes I want to play in bed, and that’s where my Steam Deck comes in (my little brother sold it to me, and the little fool still regrets it). It’s a Linux PC with the best controller known to man attached to both sides, which means I can even tinker with it like a real PC, just on the go. I very occasionally play on original hardware too, and honestly the last time I felt the need was Mother 3, since its music‑based battle system only avoids lag on the real thing. Even then, my 2DS is allegedly less laggy than a real GBA, but hunting down a GBA plus a pirated English‑patched copy isn’t worth the trouble.
    content paragraph 2: I don’t think of my desk as the “real” way to game — I just like playing each game in what I consider its ideal setup. But sometimes I want to play a game just a bit more than I want to experience it in the perfect way on my desktop PC, so I weigh the options and pick whatever space makes sense in that moment. I don’t do couch gaming at all, and if I leave my room it’s never for gaming (unless I’m at a computer store, but that doesn’t count). Gaming outside my room does happen, but only as a way to pass time — I’m not completely stuck in my room, even if that might be a fun life. Maybe I’d be more open to the idea if I had a nice TV in my room, but for now everything stays in my little ecosystem of desk, bed, and handheld.
    content paragraph 3: My room is also where I get excited about the future of how I’ll play. I can’t wait for the new Steam Controller — I’ve been using my Steam Deck as a controller for over a year now while waiting for Valve to finally make the controller they’ve clearly always wanted to build. Steam Input has completely changed how I game, and it’s wild how gyro aim has made my mouse feel obsolete in most games. And once Linux PSVR2 support gets ironed out, I want to dive back into VR and feel that higher level of immersion again. If the drivers still aren’t finished in a year, I’ll cave and run it through a Windows 11 VM, but either way, all of that tinkering and experimenting is something that only really happens in my room — it’s the space where my setup evolves.

HOW:
    h2: How can I PC Game?
    content paragraph 1: PC gaming, for me, is about finding the way a game should feel — not proving a point, not being different, and definitely not joining the endless “best input method” debates. I refine my setups because I genuinely enjoy making games play better, not because I’m trying to escape the norm. Steam Input didn’t just change how I play; it felt like the natural evolution of controls, the moment everything finally clicked into place. Gyro aim gives me a level of precision and comfort that feels right for me, just like mouse and keyboard feels right for others. And honestly, both sides can get weirdly defensive — controller players and mouse players alike — when it should really be a conversation about making gaming more accessible, more flexible, and more enjoyable for everyone. The fun part is perfecting the control scheme, dialing in the settings, and shaping the experience so each game plays the way it deserves to.
    content paragraph 2: When I start a game, I usually reach for my Steam Deck‑as‑a‑controller unless the game is built around the mouse, like point‑and‑clicks. If a game is d‑pad centric — mostly 2D stuff — I grab my Super Nintendo controller, even if that means getting creative to make it work. My choices always come down to a mix of precision, comfort, immersion, and what the game actually demands. A controller (with gyro, to me at least) often feels more immersive because it’s more expressive and precise, and the fact that so many shooters pair stick‑only camera controls with heavy aim assist says a lot about the limitations of that input method. Steam Input is where everything comes together: I tweak every game, I refine every layout, and even though I have loose templates for certain genres, it always takes playtesting to get it perfect — and I love that process. My desktop is usually the best place to play, but some games deserve the comfort of my bed or the convenience of being a passtime between things, which is where the Steam Deck shines as a handheld. Original hardware is reserved for rare edge cases like Mother 3, where timing or latency actually matters. And VR is something I see as the future — maybe even the standard one day — but right now it’s more of an experimental glimpse at what full sensory immersion could be. If I ever get frustrated at a game for not supporting my controller properly, it’s only because I know the fix wouldn’t take much work, and the experience could be so much better for everyone.
    content paragraph 3: At the end of the day, the way I play is shaped by a simple idea: PC gaming gives you the freedom to make every game feel better than it did by default. It’s not about choosing sides or arguing over which input method is “correct,” because every method has strengths and weaknesses, and every player values different things. What matters to me is that the tools exist to refine the experience — to make controls more expressive, to make movement feel natural, to make aiming feel intuitive, to make the whole thing more immersive. Steam Input, gyro, handheld play, original hardware, VR… they’re all just different ways of reaching the same goal: shaping the game so it plays the way it should. And when a game doesn’t support that level of refinement, it’s frustrating not because I’m picky, but because I know how little it would take to elevate the experience for everyone. PC gaming is at its best when it embraces flexibility, experimentation, and the idea that players should be able to interact with games in the way that feels right to them — not the way tradition says they should.

2) How to include the full conversation without violating assignment rules
Your instructor’s rules boil down to:

The page must be self‑contained.

You cannot require the grader to navigate away from your hobby site.

You can include additional content inside your site, as long as it doesn’t break the layout or distract from the required sections.

So the safest, simplest, rule‑compliant way to include the entire conversation is:

Add a collapsible <details> element inside the AI Prompts section.
This keeps everything on the same page, doesn’t require navigation, and doesn’t violate any assignment rule because:

<details> is native HTML

It doesn’t require external links

It doesn’t force the grader to scroll through a giant wall of text unless they choose to

It keeps your required content clean and visible

It still counts as “included on the page”

What it looks like:
html

  <h2>AI Prompts</h2>

  <p>I used AI only for images and for discussing how to include the video in the How section.</p>

  <details>
    <summary>Full Conversation Transcript (click to expand)</summary>
    <p>…paste the entire conversation here…</p>
  </details>
</section>
This is 100% valid HTML, fully accessible, and keeps everything on your page.

Why this is the best option
No external navigation

No iframe embedding of another site

No PDF downloads

No JavaScript hacks

No broken assignment rules

Clean, collapsible, and optional for the grader

You can paste as much text as you want inside <details> without breaking anything

Don’t link to Copilot

Don’t embed Copilot

Don’t require the grader to leave your site

Don’t hide it behind a button that loads external content

Don’t put it in a separate HTML file (they said “single page”)

is there a simple way to copy this whole convo or do I have to manually select big pieces?
You don’t have a one‑click “export conversation” button in Copilot right now, so there isn’t a hidden trick you’re missing. The interface is built around scrolling history, not bulk copying. That leaves you with three practical ways to grab the whole transcript, each with different trade‑offs depending on how long the conversation is and how clean you want the result to be.

The three workable approaches
Manual select + copy is the most reliable because it preserves the exact text, but it’s tedious for long chats. The browser will let you scroll up, click at the top, hold Shift, click at the bottom, and copy everything in one go. It’s still manual, but you don’t have to drag the whole way.

Split‑copying in chunks is the fallback when the chat is extremely long and the browser struggles with selecting everything at once. You copy a few screens at a time and paste them into a text file, then drop that into your <details> block.

Print‑to‑PDF → copy text from the PDF works surprisingly well. You open the browser’s print dialog, choose “Save as PDF,” then copy the text from the PDF. It keeps the order intact and avoids selection glitches.

Why there isn’t a simpler built‑in method
The chat UI isn’t designed as an exportable transcript tool, so it doesn’t expose a “download conversation” feature. That’s why the safest method is still copying the text yourself so you know it’s complete and accurate.

What works best for your assignment
Since you’re going to paste the transcript into a <details> element on your hobby site, the cleanest workflow is:

Scroll to the very top of the conversation.

Click at the start of the first message.

Scroll to the bottom.

Shift‑click at the end of the last message.

Copy → paste into your AI Prompts section.

This gives you the full transcript in one shot without relying on me to reconstruct anything.

If you want, I can generate the exact <details> block you’ll paste it into so you don’t have to think about formatting while you’re copying.

            color: var(--accent);
            text-decoration: none;
        }

        footer .firm a:hover {
            text-shadow: 0 0 6px var(--accent), 0 0 12px var(--accent);
        }

            }
            section {
                padding: 1.25rem;
            }
        }
    </style>

    <!-- Minimal JS for SPA-like navigation -->
    <script>
        function showSection(id) {
            document.querySelectorAll("main section").forEach(sec => {
                sec.style.display = "none";
            });
            const target = document.getElementById(id);
            if (target) {
                target.style.display = "block";
                window.scrollTo({ top: 0, behavior: "smooth" });
            }
        }
    </script>
</head>

    <!-- WHAT (Home) -->
    <section id="what">
        <h2>My Awesome Hobby: PC Gaming</h2>

        <p>
            PC gaming is genuinely the ultimate way to play games for me. A lot of people get overwhelmed by all
            the settings and sliders, but I actually enjoy configuring everything and making a game run exactly
            how I want. That applies to retro games too—between emulation, PC ports, and community projects, you
            can customize older titles in ways that weren’t possible on original hardware, and I even hook a CRT
            TV directly to my PC because many 90s games were designed around that display technology. After all
            the setup, I finally sit down and play, and it feels like I’m experiencing the game in the best
            possible way.
        </p>

        <p>
            When I think of PC gaming, I see it as an open platform built around freedom and choice. You can
            decide exactly what settings you run your games at, where you buy them, what hardware you use, and
            even what operating system you prefer—Linux now runs most games out of the box. PC gaming also gives
            you the variety you expect from any platform, from online multiplayer without a subscription to any
            genre you can imagine, all on whatever display you want. And because the PC supports emulation, I can
            legally dump and play the games I own using the tools I prefer, which adds another layer of flexibility
            I really enjoy.
        </p>

        <p>
            Building a computer can be a big barrier for some people, which is why many just buy a pre‑built, but
            I genuinely enjoy putting together part lists and seeing how different components work together. Any
            time a new console launches, I’m immediately curious about how it stacks up against modern PC hardware
            and what design choices the developers made. I love analyzing how console makers and game studios
            optimize their games for specific hardware, and channels like Digital Foundry dive deep into exactly
            that kind of comparison. Following this kind of technical breakdown is a big part of what makes PC
            gaming exciting for me.
        </p>

        <figure>
            <!-- IMAGE: neon-lit PC + CRT (place in images/ folder and update src) -->
            <img src="images/pc-crt-neon.png" alt="A neon-lit PC tower next to a CRT displaying retro visuals.">
            <figcaption>A neon‑lit PC tower next to a CRT displaying retro visuals.</figcaption>
        </figure>
        <p>
            <em>
                I generated this image using the exact words I asked the AI: “a neon‑lit PC tower with a CRT
                showing pixel art,” with the clarification that the pixel art should be the kind that blends
                together on a CRT to almost no longer look like pixel art.
            </em>
        </p>
    </section>

    <!-- WHO -->
    <section id="who">
        <h2>Who I Am: Amir Homsi</h2>

        <p>
            I’ve been playing on computers in some form since I was about five. I detoured into iPads and iOS
            during the latter half of elementary school, but because Apple locked down so many gaming features,
            I ended up tinkering with those devices the same way I would a computer. Growing up, I watched tons
            of YouTube let’s plays, which helped me explore all kinds of games even though I could only play
            maybe a quarter of them on whatever hardware I had at the time—an Xbox 360 late in its generation,
            a Wii U with mostly Nintendo titles, and older systems that could only run certain retro games. As
            I got older and gained access to better hardware, I finally got to play more games than I watched,
            and now I try to play—or at least form an opinion on—as many games as I can.
        </p>

        <p>
            It took me a surprisingly long time to realize that computer science was the field I’d been orbiting
            my whole life. The childhood dream of game development, jailbreaking my iPad, and homebrewing my 2DS
            were all connected, but the wires didn’t click until middle school, when I finally learned that being
            a doctor was nothing like the three video games that made it look fun. Once I turned my attention
            toward computers directly, they stopped being just the middleman for entertainment and became the
            entertainment themselves. Sometimes I wonder how much deeper into this hobby I would’ve gone if I had
            figured it out earlier—would I be some kind of super‑genius by now???
        </p>

        <p>
            I decided to study computer science at UNCC mostly because it was affordable and close to home, not
            because of some dramatic lifelong dream. Even so, I’ve learned a lot here—especially compared to high
            school, where most classes felt like a snooze fest except for math and a few odd exceptions. I’m
            naturally curious and inquisitive, so sometimes I wonder how much further along I’d be if I’d had real
            CS classes earlier, maybe even enough to land a job in this chaotic job market already. And for the
            record, if any employer ever reads this, my lack of club involvement isn’t because I couldn’t be
            bothered—there just wasn’t a group that felt like the right place for me, and I would’ve absolutely
            killed it if there had been.
        </p>

        <figure>
            <!-- IMAGE: real photo of you (place in images/ folder and update src) -->
            <img src="images/amir-mgs4-face.png" alt="Me doing my best impression of the American Metal Gear Solid 4 box art.">
            <figcaption>Me, doing my best impression of the (American) Metal Gear Solid 4 box art. Yeah, I know it was not very good.</figcaption>
        </figure>
        <p>
            <em>

    </section>

    <!-- WHEN -->
    <section id="when">
        <h2>When I Dive Into PC Gaming</h2>

        <p>
            I used to play for hours every day, especially back in middle school. Nowadays there are plenty of
            days where I don’t play a single game, and that honestly saddens me a bit. But I’m also glad I respect
            gaming enough to want each session to feel complete, not something that gets arbitrarily cut short.
            I try to play only when I feel I can give whatever game I’m in the middle of the attention it deserves.
        </p>

        <p>
            I also pick the right kind of game for the time I have and where I am in life. I can’t grind an Elden
            Ring boss if I’m lying in bed or sitting in a waiting room, but I can play something like Metroid or
            Silksong in those moments. I make time to play with friends too, and that usually creates a safe,
            dedicated block of time where the game gets my full attention. Some games even feel like they require
            a specific controller, especially retro ones, and I won’t play them unless I have the right setup.
        </p>

        <p>
            There are also stretches where I don’t end up gaming, but it’s never because I need time away from
            screens — my brain is basically flipped from people who feel that way. Sometimes I play because it
            fits into a routine my mind is stuck in, even outside of being productive; booting a game or beating
            a small section can feel like a tiny goal I set for myself. Other times I’m just not in the right
            headspace for a specific game, and I wait until the moment feels right to return. When I do come back,
            it feels intentional, like I picked the exact time that makes the experience feel satisfying instead
            of rushed.
        </p>

        <figure>
            <!-- IMAGE: scale “finish that game / start that other game” -->
            <img src="images/finish-start-scale.png" alt="A scale leaning between 'finish that game' and 'start that other game'.">
            <figcaption>A scale leaning between “finish that game” and “start that other game,” showing how my gaming time often swings between the two.</figcaption>
        </figure>
        <p>
            <em>
                I generated this image by asking the LLM I used for “a scale with two sides labeled ‘finish that
                game’ and ‘start that other game,’ with the scale leaning toward the latter to show how I go back
                and forth on what to play.”
            </em>
        </p>
    </section>

    <!-- WHERE -->
    <section id="where">
        <h2>Where Can I PC Game?</h2>

        <p>
            I usually play games at my desk, because that’s my battle station — if that wasn’t my go‑to spot,
            something would be seriously wrong. But sometimes I want to play in bed, and that’s where my Steam
            Deck comes in (my little brother sold it to me, and the little fool still regrets it). It’s a Linux
            PC with the best controller known to man attached to both sides, which means I can even tinker with
            it like a real PC, just on the go. I very occasionally play on original hardware too, and honestly
            the last time I felt the need was Mother 3, since its music‑based battle system only avoids lag on
            the real thing. Even then, my 2DS is allegedly less laggy than a real GBA, but hunting down a GBA
            plus a pirated English‑patched copy isn’t worth the trouble.
        </p>

        <p>
            I don’t think of my desk as the “real” way to game — I just like playing each game in what I consider
            its ideal setup. But sometimes I want to play a game just a bit more than I want to experience it in
            the perfect way on my desktop PC, so I weigh the options and pick whatever space makes sense in that
            moment. I don’t do couch gaming at all, and if I leave my room it’s never for gaming (unless I’m at
            a computer store, but that doesn’t count). Gaming outside my room does happen, but only as a way to
            pass time — I’m not completely stuck in my room, even if that might be a fun life. Maybe I’d be more
            open to the idea if I had a nice TV in my room, but for now everything stays in my little ecosystem
            of desk, bed, and handheld.
        </p>

        <p>
            My room is also where I get excited about the future of how I’ll play. I can’t wait for the new Steam
            Controller — I’ve been using my Steam Deck as a controller for over a year now while waiting for Valve
            to finally make the controller they’ve clearly always wanted to build. Steam Input has completely
            changed how I game, and it’s wild how gyro aim has made my mouse feel obsolete in most games. And once
            Linux PSVR2 support gets ironed out, I want to dive back into VR and feel that higher level of immersion
            again. If the drivers still aren’t finished in a year, I’ll cave and run it through a Windows 11 VM,
            but either way, all of that tinkering and experimenting is something that only really happens in my
            room — it’s the space where my setup evolves.
        </p>

        <figure>
            <!-- IMAGE: stylized L-shaped setup -->
            <img src="images/l-shaped-setup.png" alt="A stylized version of my actual L-shaped gaming setup with PC, CRT, Steam Deck, and audio gear.">
            <figcaption>A stylized version of my actual L‑shaped gaming setup, including my PC, CRT, Steam Deck, and audio gear.</figcaption>
        </figure>
        <p>
            <em>
                I generated this image by asking the LLM for a stylized recreation of my real L‑shaped gaming setup,
                including my 27‑inch monitor, RGB PC, CRT angled toward me, Steam Deck, SNES controller, Roccat mouse,
                and my audio interface with a Shure mic and Sennheiser headphones.
            </em>
        </p>
    </section>

    <!-- HOW -->
    <section id="how">
        <h2>How Can I PC Game?</h2>

        <p>
            PC gaming, for me, is about finding the way a game should feel — not proving a point, not being
            different, and definitely not joining the endless “best input method” debates. I refine my setups
            because I genuinely enjoy making games play better, not because I’m trying to escape the norm. Steam
            Input didn’t just change how I play; it felt like the natural evolution of controls, the moment
            everything finally clicked into place. Gyro aim gives me a level of precision and comfort that feels
            right for me, just like mouse and keyboard feels right for others. And honestly, both sides can get
            weirdly defensive — controller players and mouse players alike — when it should really be a conversation
            about making gaming more accessible, more flexible, and more enjoyable for everyone. The fun part is
            perfecting the control scheme, dialing in the settings, and shaping the experience so each game plays
            the way it deserves to.
        </p>

        <p>
            When I start a game, I usually reach for my Steam Deck‑as‑a‑controller unless the game is built around
            the mouse, like point‑and‑clicks. If a game is d‑pad centric — mostly 2D stuff — I grab my Super
            Nintendo controller, even if that means getting creative to make it work. My choices always come down
            to a mix of precision, comfort, immersion, and what the game actually demands. A controller (with gyro,
            to me at least) often feels more immersive because it’s more expressive and precise, and the fact that
            so many shooters pair stick‑only camera controls with heavy aim assist says a lot about the limitations
            of that input method. Steam Input is where everything comes together: I tweak every game, I refine every
            layout, and even though I have loose templates for certain genres, it always takes playtesting to get it
            perfect — and I love that process. My desktop is usually the best place to play, but some games deserve
            the comfort of my bed or the convenience of being a passtime between things, which is where the Steam
            Deck shines as a handheld. Original hardware is reserved for rare edge cases like Mother 3, where timing
            or latency actually matters. And VR is something I see as the future — maybe even the standard one day —
            but right now it’s more of an experimental glimpse at what full sensory immersion could be. If I ever get
            frustrated at a game for not supporting my controller properly, it’s only because I know the fix wouldn’t
            take much work, and the experience could be so much better for everyone.
        </p>

        <p>
            At the end of the day, the way I play is shaped by a simple idea: PC gaming gives you the freedom to
            make every game feel better than it did by default. It’s not about choosing sides or arguing over which
            input method is “correct,” because every method has strengths and weaknesses, and every player values
            different things. What matters to me is that the tools exist to refine the experience — to make controls
            more expressive, to make movement feel natural, to make aiming feel intuitive, to make the whole thing
            more immersive. Steam Input, gyro, handheld play, original hardware, VR… they’re all just different ways
            of reaching the same goal: shaping the game so it plays the way it should. And when a game doesn’t support
            that level of refinement, it’s frustrating not because I’m picky, but because I know how little it would
            take to elevate the experience for everyone. PC gaming is at its best when it embraces flexibility,
            experimentation, and the idea that players should be able to interact with games in the way that feels
            right to them — not the way tradition says they should.
        </p>

        <figure>
            <!-- YOUTUBE VIDEO EMBED -->
            <iframe
                width="560"
                height="315"
                src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/binPB4YbWmM"
                title="YouTube video player"
                allow="accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture; web-share"
                allowfullscreen>
            </iframe>
            <figcaption>
                A video essay that shaped how I think about input methods, motion controls, and the future of how we interact with games.
            </figcaption>
        </figure>
        <p>
            <em>
                I discussed how I would include this video in the section because it reflects many of the ideas I
                talk about here and helped shape my perspective on control design and PC gaming.
            </em>
        </p>
    </section>

    <!-- WHY -->
    <section id="why">
        <h2>Why PC Gaming?</h2>

        <p>
            PC gaming is the closest thing gaming has to being truly open source. The entire world quietly runs on
            open‑source software, and most people don’t realize just how much of everything they use depends on it.
            I love PC gaming because it shares that same spirit—when a hobby is open, people can build wild, creative
            things that would never exist in a locked‑down ecosystem. Imagine if every CG asset in your favorite
            action movie were available for cheap or even free, or if you could build an original car from parts sold
            by BMW, Toyota, or even Ferrari; that’s the kind of possibility openness creates.
        </p>

        <p>
            Another reason I care so much about PC gaming is that there’s always one layer deeper—there’s always more
            to the story. Any time I tweak a setting, mod a game, or fix something that breaks, it usually confuses me
            at first, but I often end up learning how all these pieces manage to play nice… or sometimes don’t. It’s a
            hobby that gets better the more you do it, and honestly, it even improves passively over time just by being
            around it.
        </p>

        <p>
            I also care about PC gaming because it’s one of the few hobbies that actually grows with you instead of
            aging out (the RAM prices will go down, high framerates and resolutions will become affordable, just have
            faith!). The games I played as a kid still run today—sometimes better than ever—and the hardware I use now
            will still matter years from now in some form. There’s something comforting about a hobby that I’ll fight
            to keep around, one that doesn’t vanish when the trend cycle moves on but keeps expanding, layering new
            experiences on top of old ones without replacing them.
        </p>

        <figure>
            <!-- IMAGE: four-quadrant heart -->
            <img src="images/four-heart.png" alt="A four-quadrant heart in Nintendo red, Sony blue, Microsoft green, and Valve orange-red.">
            <figcaption>A four‑quadrant heart representing Nintendo, Valve, Sony, and Microsoft—pretend their logos are in each quadrant respectively.</figcaption>
        </figure>
        <p>
            <em>
                I generated this image by asking the LLM I used for “a heart divided into four equal quadrants, each
                one colored to represent Nintendo (red), Sony (blue), Microsoft (green), and Valve (gray), with no
                logos — just the colors.” I originally wanted the actual company logos in each quadrant, but the LLM
                I used told me that would be a copyright/trademark issue, so we used color‑only quadrants instead.
            </em>
        </p>
    </section>
